The standard German edition of Hegelโs collected works is the twenty-volume Jubilee Edition, edited by H. Glockner and published 
in Stuttgart from 1927 to 1930. For the English-speaking reader wondering where to begin reading Hegelโs own works, I would 
recommend following the order of the works discussed in this book. English translations of these works are: [3mLectures on the 
Philosophy of History, [0mtr. J. Sibree (Dover, New York, 1956). The introduction to these lectures is available separately, as [3m
Reason in History: A General Introduction to the Philosophy of History [0m, tr. R. S. Hartman (Library of Liberal Arts, New York, 
1953), and as [3mLectures on the Philosophy of World History [0m. [3mIntroduction: Reason in History, [0mtr. H. B. Nisbet (Cambridge 
University Press, Cambridge, 1975). The latter edition contains additional scholarly material. 

[3mHegelโs Philosophy of Right, [0mtr. T. M. Knox (Oxford University Press, London, 1967). 

[3mThe Phenomenology of Mind, [0mtr. J. B. Baillie (Harper & Row, New York, 1967). There is also a more recent and, in the view of many, 
more reliable translation by A. V. Miller, entitled [3mHegelโs Phenomenology of Spirit [0m(Oxford University Press, Oxford, 1977). 

[3mHegelโs Science of Logic, [0mtr. W. H. Johnston and L. G. Struthers in two volumes (Allen & Unwin, London, 1929); also more recently 
translated by A. V. Miller (Allen & Unwin, London, 1969). 

Probably the most important of Hegelโs other works is his [3mEncyclopedia of the Philosophical Sciences [0m. This work has been 
published in English in its separate components. The section on logic, sometimes called the โLesser Logicโ, was translated by W. 
Wallace and published under the confusing title [3mThe Logic of Hegel [0m(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1874). There is another edition, with 
a foreword by J. N. Findlay (Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1975). The second part is available as [3mHegelโs Philosophy of Nature [0m, tr. A. 
V. Miller (Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1970) and also in a translation by M. J. Petry (Allen & Unwin, London, 1970). Wallaceโs 
original translation of [3mHegelโs Philosophy of Mind [0m(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1894) has been republished, together with additional 
material translated by A. V. Miller (Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1971). 

English translations of Hegelโs other writings are: [3mEarly Theological Writings, [0mtr. T. M. Knox (University of Chicago Press, 
Chicago, 1948). This volume contains the earliest of Hegelโs surviving works. 

[3mHegelโs Political Writings, [0mtr. T. M. Knox (Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1964). Hegelโs occasional political essays, such as that on 
the English Reform Bill, are collected here. 

[3mLectures on the Philosophy of Religion, [0mtr. E. B. Speirs and J. B. Sanderson, in three volumes (Routledge & Kegan Paul, London, 
1968). 

[3mHegelโs Aesthetics, [0mtr. T. M. Knox, in two volumes (Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1975). 

[3mLectures on the History of Philosophy, [0mtr. E. S. Haldane and F. H. Simson, in three volumes (Routledge & Kegan Paul, London, 
1955). 

For a convenient selection of Hegelโs writings, with an extensive bibliography, see [3mThe Hegel Reader [0mby Georg Wilhelm Friedrich 
Hegel, edited by Stephen Houlgate (Blackwell, Oxford, 1998).

For readers wishing for further guidance, I would recommend Richard Normanโs slim and stimulating volume, [3mHegelโs Phenomenology: A 
Philosophical Introduction [0m(Sussex University Press, Brighton, 1976). Next could come Ivan Soll, [3mAn Introduction to Hegelโs 
Metaphysics [0m(University of Chicago Press, Chicago, 1969). After that one might move on to the shorter of Charles Taylorโs two 
books on Hegel, [3mHegel and Modern Society [0m(Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1979), or, if one is ready for it, to the much 
longer and more difficult [3mHegel [0m(Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1975). For a detailed study of Hegelโs [3mPhenomenology [0m, see 
Robert Solomon, [3mIn the Spirit of Hegel: A Study of Hegelโs Phenomenology [0m(Oxford University Press, New York, 1983); H. S. Harris, [3m
Hegelโs Ladder: A Commentary on Hegelโs Phenomenology of Spirit [0m(Hackett, Indianapolis, 1995); and Michael N. Forster, [3mHegelโs 
Idea of a Phenomenology of Spirit [0m(University of Chicago Press, Chicago, 1998). 

On Hegelโs life, see [3mHegel: A Biography [0mby Terry P. Pinkard (Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 2000). The two volumes [3mHegelโs 
Development: Towards the Sunlight, 1770โ1801 [0mand [3mHegelโs Development: Night Thoughts (Jena 1801โ1806) [0mby H. S. Harris (Clarendon 
Press, Oxford, 1972, 1983) is a fine study of Hegelโs early years. The influential work by George Lukacs, [3mThe Young Hegel, [0mis 
available in an English translation by R. Livingstone (Merlin Press, London, 1975). 

Tom Rockmore puts Hegelโs theory of knowledge into its historical context in [3mBefore and After Hegel: A Historical Introduction to 
Hegelโs Thought [0m(University of California, Berkeley and Los Angeles, 1993). Older, but still useful works are: Edward Caird, [3mHegel [0m
(Blackwood, Edinburgh, 1901); Benedetto Croce, [3mWhat is Living and What is Dead in the Philosophy of Hegel?, [0mtr. D. Ainslie 
(Russell & Russell, New York, 1969); W. T. Stace, [3mThe Philosophy of Hegel [0m(Dover, New York, 1955); and J. N. Findlay, [3mHegel: A 
Re-examination [0m(Allen & Unwin, London, 1958). 

Among notable books on Hegelโs social and political ideas are: Herbert Marcuse, [3mReason and Revolution [0m(Humanities Press, New York, 
1954); Shlomo Avineri, [3mHegelโs Theory of the Modern State [0m(Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1972); Raymond Plant, [3mHegel [0m
(Allen & Unwin, London, 1973); and Judith Shklar, [3mFreedom and Independence: A Study of the Political Ideas of Hegelโs 
โPhenomenology of Mindโ [0m(Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1976). Some heated exchanges over the extent to which Hegelโs 
philosophy supports the authoritarian state have been collected by Walter Kaufmann in a volume entitled [3mHegelโs Political 
Philosophy [0m(Atherton Press, New York, 1970). There is also a collection of articles edited by Z. Pelczynski, entitled [3mHegelโs 
Political Philosophy [0m(Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1971). Alan Pattenโs [3mHegelโs Idea of Freedom [0m(Oxford University 
Press, New York, 1999) gives an accessible account of Hegelโs social and political philosophy. Together with this one might 
readโthough with considerable cautionโKarl Popperโs provocative attempt to find the origins of totalitarianism in Hegelโs thought: 
see [3mThe Open Society and Its Enemies [0m, vol. II, chapter 12 (Routledge & Kegan Paul, London, 1966). 

For further study of the religious aspect of Hegelโs thought, see Emil Fackenheim, [3mThe Religious Dimension of Hegelโs Thought [0m
(Indiana University Press, Bloomington, 1967), and Bernard Reardon, [3mHegelโs Philosophy of Religion [0m(Macmillan, London, 1977). See 
also Robert C. Whittemoreโs article, โHegel as Panentheistโ in [3mTulane Studies in Philosophy [0m, vol. IX (1960), pp. 134โ64. This 
volume is a special issue on Hegel. 

There are innumerable scholarly articles on Hegel in the philosophical journals. Some of the best have been collected in Alasdair 
MacIntyre (ed.), [3mHegel: A Collection of Critical Essays [0m(Anchor, New York, 1972). [3mThe Cambridge Companion to Hegel, [0medited by 
Frederick C. Beiser (Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1993) is useful for the serious student of Hegel. 

Finally, the various transformations of Hegelโs thought by the Young Hegelians are well described in Sidney Hook, [3mFrom Hegel to 
Marx [0m(Humanities Press, New York, 1958), and also in David McLellan, [3mThe Young Hegelians and Karl Marx [0m(Macmillan, London, 1969).
